#### Cash flows in the valuation model
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the tasks that you'll probably run into if you're trying to build a financial model is the need to value the firm.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=8)** This is going to start with determining what the cash flows for that firm are going to be.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=13)** Now, that sounds simple enough, right?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=14)** We could just look at the statement of cash flows.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=17)** But the reality is, it's a bit more tricky than you might think.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=20)** I'm in the 04_02_Begin Excel file.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=24)** Now, what we've got here is our three-statement financial model, and I focused on the statement of cash flows as well as our various assumptions over time.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=34)** In order to evaluate the value of this firm, we're going to need to determine the free cash flow for the company.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=45)** The free cash flow is going to be driven by the cash from operations for the firm, less what the firm thinks it's going to need to spend on capital expenditures.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=57)** This is one of the assumptions that we'll often have to make in order to build an effective valuation model.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=65)** So in this case, the firm is projecting cash from operations of 30,215.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=71)** We've got cap ex of an additional 6,176.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=76)** We can take this and adjust it across all five years, and that'll give us our free cash flow over that five-year period of time.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=86)** But, if we really want to understand the value of the firm, we're going to have to go one step beyond this.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=93)** We're going to have to look at what the present value of these free cash flows is.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=99)** So, to do that, we're going to use the present value formula in Excel, which is simply =PV open parentheses, then the discount rate that we'll use.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=113)** We'll add that in just a moment.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=115)** The number of periods in the future that the cash flows will be received.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=120)** So in this case, the easiest way to handle that, is going to be to take the year, and then subtract off the current year.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=130)** PMT, or payment, would be any additional dividends we get.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=134)** I'll assume it's nothing, and then our future value is going to be our free cash flow.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=139)** But, for Excel, we're going to have to put a negative in front of that free cash flow, because it would be a cash outflow when it gets paid to us.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=147)** And then finally, we'll say this is at the end of the year in question.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=151)** Now, Excel's not going to help us until we add in that discount rate.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=156)** The discount rate is based on the riskiness of the firm.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=160)** So I'm going to use a seven percent discount rate here.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=164)** That will be a reasonable starting point.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=166)** So now, Excel will go through and help us to determine what the value of this particular set of cash flows is.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=177)** Now if I've done my job properly, we'll be able to go through and take that discount rate, apply it overtime, and use this same formula with only minimal changes over time.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=193)** And if we widen out each of our columns, we'll now be able to determine what the present value of all of our future cash flows is based on the year, the discount rate, and the cash flow itself.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/cash-flows-in-the-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=210)** And this is the first step down the road towards figuring out the valuation of that firm.

#### Terminal value in a valuation model
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When valuing a company, a key part of the valuation is not just the cash flows that'll be generated for the next few years, but ultimately, what the company is going to be worth far down the road, what we call the terminal value.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=14)** I'm in the 04_03_Begin Excel file.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=19)** Now, we're going through and determining the value for this particular firm.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=24)** We've already figured out that the present value of its free cash flows are over the next few years.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=29)** The firm also has $5,000 in free cash on its balance sheet that we can use as part of our evaluation.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=37)** What we need to figure out though is how much that firm is going to be worth in 2024 and beyond.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=44)** And this is where the concept of the terminal value comes in.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=48)** So the terminal value is simply going to be equal to the previous year's free cashflow times one plus the growth rate.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=60)** Now, we're going to have to make an assumption about what our long term growth in this company is going to be.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=66)** In this case, we've assumed it's 5% for the year 2024 and beyond.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=72)** In other words, how fast will that company grow in perpetuity for the long run?
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=77)** We'll assume it's 5%.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=81)** Finally, we're going to have to go through and divide by the discount rate minus our growth rate.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=90)** And doing that then gives us the terminal value.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=94)** So in this particular case, we see that the firm is worth, after adjusting, roughly $2,054,105.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=108)** Now, that's the value in 2024, but we're not especially interested in the value in 2024, we want to know about the value today.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=117)** So to figure that out, we're going to need to use our old friend, the present value formula.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=122)** So I'll take that from the previous cell and we'll apply it here to our terminal value.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=131)** So, first we need to determine our rate.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=133)** Our rate is our discount rate.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=135)** We're going to leave that the same.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=137)** Next, we need to know how far in the future that terminal value occurs.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=141)** Well, it's in 2024.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=144)** At present, in the model, it's 2018, so we'll use six years down the road.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=150)** We're getting no payment beyond that terminal value.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=154)** The terminal value is our future value, and that'll be received at the end of that 2024 year.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=160)** So what's the present value here?
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=162)** Well, it's going to be $1,368,736, and I'll clean this up by taking away the decimal places.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=172)** To do this, if you're wondering how I do that so fast, I hold down the Control key and hit the number one.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=179)** Now, we're ready to go through and determine the total value of the firm based on all of these different components.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=189)** This is what we refer to as the discounted cashflow model.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=194)** This is simply going to be the sum of all of these individual discounted cash flows for the period from 2018 at present through 2024.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=205)** And this will tell us that the intrinsic value of the firm is $1.501 million.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/terminal-value-in-a-valuation-model-2?u=76281980&t=213)** Now you're ready to apply a DCF model to your own financial forecasts.

#### Interpreting a DCF model
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to financial models built around the three different major financial statements, we'll often to be asked to build financial models around stock price data and the associated financials.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=14)** Now, two types of models that we might be asked to build are the DDM, or Dividend Discount Model, and the DCF model, or Discounted Cashflow Model.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=25)** I'm in the 04_04_Begin Excel spreadsheet.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=29)** Now what we have here is stock price data for Microsoft, along with different financial metrics for the firm, including various multiples and the firm's financials over the last few years.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=42)** We want to figure out the intrinsic value of Microsoft stock.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=46)** To do that, we'll use both a DDM and a DCF.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=51)** To begin with, with the Dividend Discount Model, we're going to assume a payout ratio of 50%.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=57)** So 50% of the firm's net income will be paid out as a dividend.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=61)** We look at net income over the last 12 months and divide by the number of shares outstanding, then multiply by the payout ratio.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=71)** In order to determine the value of the stock based on this, we'll need to pick a discount rate and a growth rate.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=79)** Perhaps that should 11%, 13%, whatever we feel is appropriate, given the riskiness of Microsoft stock.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=86)** To come up with our DDM figure, we are going to take the dividend that we expect to be paid out, multiply that by one plus our growth rate, and then divide the whole thing by the discount rate minus our growth rate.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=107)** And in this case, the DDM indicates Microsoft should be valued at about $84.23.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=114)** We could adjust this up or down based on the discount rate in question, but notice, small changes in the discount rate make a big change in the value of the stock.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=125)** So how about the DCF?
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=126)** Maybe this will give us a more precise estimate.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=129)** Well, we've gone through and projected the free cashflows over the next few years for Microsoft based on its historical net income.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=138)** In order to get our DCF, in this case, we are going to use the NPV formula, or Net Present Value formula.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=147)** This starts by picking a discount rate.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=150)** We'll use 11% in this case.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=152)** Ideally, we'd want it to be consistent between the two different models, but for simplicity, we'll use 11.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=158)** Now we're going to go through and pick each of the values for the cash that can be paid out to us as shareholders.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=168)** So in this case, that'll start initially with the net cash on the balance sheet, plus the free cashflows in each of the next few years, and then finally, the terminal value for the firm.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=182)** Now notice, we've used the NPV formula here, because we have cashflows each and every year in equal increments.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=191)** If we didn't have those increments, we've have to use the X NPV function.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=196)** So when we get done, we'll have a discounted cashflow valuation on the firm as a whole; it's $895,000.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=204)** We have one more thing left to do.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=206)** We need to divide by the number of shares outstanding.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=210)** When we do that, we get a DCF model price of $116.65.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=216)** How is that compared to where Microsoft is trading at this point in time?
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=220)** Well, when the financial model was produced, Microsoft was trading at about $114.37.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=227)** Pretty close to accurate, right?
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=228)** This would indicate that Microsoft was slightly undervalued based on these particular assumptions.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interpreting-a-dcf-model-2?u=76281980&t=234)** Now you're all set to go through and build your own financial models around stock prices given financial data.

### 6. 5. Banking Models

#### Beyond the basics in financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Modeling basic asset valuation and financial results are crucial skills for financial analysts.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=6)** But it's often useful to go beyond that level and build financial models that show the effects of more complicated deals, things like mergers and acquisitions and leveraged buyouts.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=16)** These types of advanced financial transactions are common, especially at larger companies.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=22)** When we're doing more advanced financial modeling, we're typically trying to model a more complex deal.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=28)** In particular, probably the most common areas where you see the need for advanced financial models are modeling buyouts by private equity firms and modeling merger and acquisition deals by large companies.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=40)** Sometimes there are other types of deals that come up, such as recapitalizations or go-private transactions, where advanced financial models are also needed.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=50)** These types of advanced financial models generally show cash flows far into the future and then show the actions that are dependent on those cash flows, things like paying down debt based on the level of cash that's available at a company.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=66)** In particular, three common modeling techniques that come up in more advanced financial models include Corkscrews, Toggles, and Waterfalls.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=75)** Each of these different types of techniques helps to deal with special circumstances around cash flows.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=81)** Now, advanced financial models are often not necessarily a single model, but rather multiple, simpler models linked together.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=89)** For example, a buyout model will often link together a separate page for the buyout itself plus a separate page for a three-statement model and another separate page for a DCF model.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=104)** The reason for this is that the buyout model pulls information from the projections based on the three-statement model and the DCF model.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=113)** As a result, it's important to understand how each of the simpler models works in order to understand the outputs from the more advanced model.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=122)** Buyout models give go/no-go decisions on buyouts of other firms.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=128)** In particular, this requires projections about cash flows from the investment, and it also requires making assumptions about future valuations and interest rates.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=138)** The goal here, though, is to help identify issues that may come up in the near future with the deal and then ultimately allow top-company executives to make a decision about whether or not it makes sense to move forward with that buyout.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/beyond-the-basics-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=153)** Now you should have a sense for how more advanced financial transactions can be represented through financial models.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** private (2)
> **Env Vars:** dcf (2)
> **Analogies:** such as (1), for example (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Corkscrews and models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] A common technique in building detailed financial models, is for one periods sales value to be related to the next period.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=7)** The same thing holds true with many costs, debts and other financial variables.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=13)** This relationship is called a corkscrew, and it's an important concept to be aware of in modeling.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=18)** This is an example of a buy out model.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=22)** Now, one thing that I want you to notice is that in addition to the buy out model itself, shown on this sheet, there's also a DCF model and a three statement model.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=33)** The three different models are linked together in the buy out model.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=38)** The reason for this is that the take aways from the buy out model, whether this particular investment should be made or not, requires information that is available in the DCF model, related to valuation and rate of return, and in the three statement model, related to projections about where the company is going in the future, in terms of it's sales, profits and costs.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=63)** We can observe that looking at any of these green links.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=66)** Any of the numbers in green are a link to one of the other models.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=73)** Blue is hard coded value, black is a formula, green is a link to another sheet, or a reference to another sheet.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=82)** Now, one of the things we notice, when we look down this sheet, is just how much information is drawn from other places.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=91)** What we observe, though, when we get down further into the debt and interest schedule, is the corkscrew.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=97)** Now, if this buy out commences, the acquiring company will have to take out a bank loan, a form of bank debt that is, in order to complete the buy out.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=108)** So the beginning bank debt is expected to be $64 million.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=114)** After that year, bank debt should end at 71 million $453,000.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=121)** Notice though, that 2018s ending bank debt is the same thing as 2019s starting bank debt.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=131)** The ending bank debt for 2019, is the starting bank debt for 2020, et cetera.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=138)** This creates the corkscrew effect that gives the technique it's name.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=144)** The purpose in doing this is to, number one, make sure there are no errors in the model.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=150)** Obviously we should expect to see that, everything else equal, bank debt at the end of one year should be equal to bank debt at the start of the next year.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=161)** However, the other purpose in doing all of this, is to allow us to more effectively calculate debt and see what kind of changes we could make.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=172)** We need to be able to take into account any repayments, any additional loan or interest that occurs, and then if there's going to be a default, we need to know in what year that default will occur.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/corkscrews-and-models?u=76281980&t=184)** Now you know what a corkscrew is and why it matters in modeling.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **CLI Commands:** make (2)

#### Waterfalls and models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Waterfalls are a concept used in financial modeling to help deal with how profits are split out to various buckets or stakeholders over time.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=9)** One common example of this occurs with profit distributions to bondholders, preferred stockholders, and then common stockholders.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=17)** This is an illustration of a cash flow waterfall.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=20)** So, in this case, the water, of course, is a metaphor for cash or profits from the firm, and, in this particular instance, we have our initial highest priority bucket, or the highest step in the waterfall, is for project operations and maintenance.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=37)** And you can think of this as the ongoing expenses for the company.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=40)** Right?
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=41)** When a company brings in sales, the first thing it has to do is pay to keep the lights on, pay to keep its employees there, pay for inventory, things like that.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=49)** Whatever money is left over from that can be used to pay senior debt, in particular loans from banks, from bondholders, etc.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=59)** From there, we'd probably want to build up a reserve fund or a rainy day fund.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=64)** This is often referred to as debt service reserves.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=67)** In particular, in this case, we've got debt service reserves specifically to senior debt.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=73)** So, once we've paid for all of the ongoing operations and maintenance of the company, we pay the debt that we're supposed to pay to our banks and other lenders.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=83)** Then we set aside money in the rainy day fund.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=85)** And then after that, we provide returns to the project sponsor, that is the equity investor in this particular project.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=93)** Let's take a look at how this works in Excel though.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=96)** Shall we?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=97)** Now, one of the neat things about Excel for financial modeling is that, because there's an incredibly complex amount of information that goes into a financial model, it's often useful to try and look at a visual representation for where different elements of the financial model come from.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=116)** We can do this with the formulas tab.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=119)** So if you click on the formulas tab, you'll see an option related to tracing precedence and dependence, and this will tell us where different pieces of information come from.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=129)** So, for example, our ending bank debt in 2018 comes from the beginning bank debt in 2018.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=140)** And the ending bank debt in 2019 comes from the beginning bank debt.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=146)** And we could go through and trace all of these different precedents.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=152)** And this illustrates, as an example, how the entire model comes together.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=158)** So this is an example of the corkscrew.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=161)** As you see, we have kind of a winding change in where the money comes from.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=167)** We also have an example of a waterfall here.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=170)** So I'll remove all arrows and start again.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=173)** What we see with the waterfall is that we have payments to bank debt, but then beyond those payments to bank debt, we're also going to make payments to our high yield debt that is our junior or subordinated debt, and then from there, we'd even make payments to the investors themselves.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=196)** Whatever cash we generate flows first to bank debt, then to high yield debt repayments, and, finally, down to investors at the bottom of the pyramid.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=207)** In general, any kind of major investment like this has waterfalls built into it somewhere along the way.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=215)** For example, we might have 100% of all of our cash inflows flow to senior low lenders until all of our obligations are met there.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=228)** Then, once all of our obligations to senior lenders are met, we have 100% of cash flow to junior lenders and then to preferred investors and then to equity investors.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=239)** And that's what we're illustrating here.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=241)** But you can use the tracing feature throughout any part of the financial model to get a handle on where different pieces of information come from and how they impact other pieces of information in the financial model.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=257)** This is really useful when it comes to analyzing the financial model and understanding what's going on, as well as finding any errors that might have occurred in your logic along the way.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=267)** Particularly when we get to advanced financial models, it's hard to overstate how easy it is to make an error if you're not clearly looking at the logic behind each of your cells and the formulas associated with them.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/waterfalls-and-models?u=76281980&t=282)** Now you are prepared to use waterfalls in a variety of financial modeling settings.

#### Adding toggles to a financial model
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Often times when building a financial model we need to be able to add options to adjust the model based on our expectations about the future.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=8)** A toggle is one easy way to do exactly that.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=13)** What we've added here is a toggle.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=16)** This toggle is based on our expectations about the future of this company and the economy.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=22)** So we have three different scenarios.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=24)** Scenario one is a gray sky case.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=27)** In another words if things aren't going so well for the company.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=30)** Scenario two is our base case the most likely outcomes that we foresee.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=35)** And then scenario three is our blue sky case where everything is going well.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=39)** Now this toggle is related to other parts of the sheet.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=44)** If we trace the dependents using the Formulas tab we'll see if we scroll down that the scenario directly impacts our revenue growth.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=61)** And so if we change from scenario three to scenario one for example well then our revenue shifts from being 15% in 2018 all the way down to 2.8% in 2022.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=79)** Similarly if we use scenario two now our revenue growth moves to 25% in 2018 down to 4.7% in 2022.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=93)** And scenario three gives us 35% revenue growth in 2018 and 6.6% revenue growth in 2022.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=104)** Now we can make these numbers whatever we like of course but the scenarios give us a useful starting point.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=111)** And the revenue growth ties into many other areas of the sheet.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=117)** So in particular if we scroll down revenue growth will obviously be tied to projections about revenues.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=124)** So if we scroll down we see in I66 that revenue is based on our historical revenues multiplied by our revenue growth overtime.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=138)** And of course our revenues or total sales are gonna tie into the net income that we observe at the end of the year.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=146)** So by changing just that one toggle at the top of the sheet to change what we think the scenario will be.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=155)** We can now change net income in the future across all of the years that we're projecting for the company.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=164)** The green box that I've added below illustrates net income at the end of 2022.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=172)** So if we change to scenario one our net income falls from 23.782 million to 16.175 million.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=184)** See how that works?
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=186)** Having just one toggle and linking it to other parts of the sheet lets us easily control what's going on in the model as a whole.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=194)** There are lots of different methods for adding toggles ranging from using sophisticated functions like offset to using more simple methods like I've illustrated here.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/adding-toggles-to-a-financial-model?u=76281980&t=204)** But either way now you are ready to put toggles to use in your financial models.

#### Model outputs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=0)** - One of the major outputs in buyout models and M&A models is valuation in price per share.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=6)** This is fairly easy output to generate but it's critically important for many users.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=11)** Let's take a look.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=12)** DCF models or Discounted Cash Flow models are intended to allow us to go through and evaluate the series of cash flows that a company will produce and then put a valuation on that.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=24)** The ultimate representation of that valuation is the price per share that the model represents.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=31)** This is in fact exactly the kind of financial model that professional analysts on Wall Street and investment relations advisors use when they're evaluating potential investments.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=42)** It's also something that's useful in a corporate finance setting when a company is trying to decide whether it should issue debt or equity in order to fund itself going forward.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=53)** Now on the context of an LBO or a buyout, we're interested in the valuation per share in the future versus what we could buy the shares at today.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=64)** So in our particular case, the three statement model tells us in cell E9 that the price per share for Acme Inc.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=73)** is $140 per share at present.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=76)** In our DCF model we can evaluate what the price per share will be in the future based on the assumptions that we've made.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=84)** Now there are a couple of key assumptions that we need to take a look at in order to determine what our price per share will be in the future.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=91)** First, do we want to use multiple different methods for calculating price per share or not?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=97)** In particular there are two different methods that are fairly commonly used that we might use as the bases for our model.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=106)** The first is the dividend discount model or DDM model.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=111)** This is simply based on our discount rate, our terminal growth rate, and the level of free cashflow produced by the company in the future.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=121)** As an alternative to that, we can use a simple multiples approach.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=127)** And the multiples approach is based on the EBITDA generated in the future multiplied by the EBITDA multiple that we assume the company will sell for.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=139)** Each of these different methods has their own pros and cons.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=142)** We're making assumptions to underlie each of these different methods.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=147)** In the Terminal EBITDA multiple method we're gonna be evaluating what we think companies will sell for in five years' time.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=156)** It's essentially asking, "What do you think the PE ratio will be in the sock market in five years?"
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=162)** That's tough to answer.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=164)** Maybe it should be six times, maybe it should be eight times.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=167)** It's tough to say.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=169)** We need to pick a number that we're comfortable with though.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=173)** In the dividend discount model, We're relying very heavily on our discount rate, which is a subjective criteria that we have to use based on the riskiness of the investment, and the terminal growth rate that we think the company will be able to achieve in the future.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=189)** Small changes in terminal growth rates can make a big difference in valuation.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=197)** Once we've done all that, we can decide whether or not we want to use just the DDM model, just the multiples model, or both.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=205)** In our particular case we've built a toggle here which says, "Do you want to use the multiple different methods?"
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=213)** If one, then yes.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=215)** If zero, then no.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=218)** If we don't use multiple methods we default to using just the DDM.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=222)** I'm gonna use multiple methods.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=224)** And what this model is showing us is that based on the assumptions we've made our price per share in 2022 should be $280.61.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=235)** Watch what happens if we change this terminal growth rate just a little bit though.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=239)** If we make this say a three percent growth rate in the future, price per share jumps enormously.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=245)** Goes from $280 per share to $357.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=250)** That's the other nice feature about using toggles.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=254)** At this point we're assuming scenario one, the gray sky scenario.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=260)** If we instead assume a base case scenario two, now our share price in the future jumps to almost $388 per share.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=269)** Our confidence in doing this deal and trying to capitalize on what the share price will be five years from now versus the share price today, is gonna be based on how confident we are with our assumptions.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=281)** This is the power of financial models.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=284)** We can clearly lay out where we think the company is going in the future and what assumptions we've made to get there.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/model-outputs?u=76281980&t=291)** Now you've seen how to use a financial model to establish share price valuations.

#### Hiding tabs and making models readable
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Often, financial models can get to be large and overwhelming.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=4)** But remember, the goal of model, any model, be it a map or a financial model, is to be a simplified version of reality.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=13)** To that end, it often pays to disguise some of the behind-the-scenes details that make a financial model run, but are not necessary for most users to see.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=22)** Now, this is our basic LBO model.

> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=25)** There's a few things you wanna be aware of here.

>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=45)** If we wanted to hide these tabs, that might help the ultimate user to be able to focus more on the basic LBO tab.

>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=97)** I'm gonna leave the other two tabs hidden for now.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=102)** The other thing you wanna be aware of with financial models is that complex financial models often end up having circular references.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=110)** In fact, this sheet now has a circular reference.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=114)** Let's take a look at where this came up.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=118)** So if we scroll down in the sheet, everything looks fairly normal.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=123)** But there's an indication down here at the bottom next to the word Ready that says Circular References.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=131)** We can also evaluate the model to see if there are any errors by clicking on the Formulas tab and then coming over to the Error Checking option.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=141)** When we do that, we have an option to look for circular references.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=147)** This is right next to the Trace Precedents and Trace Dependents arrows, which is also useful for understanding how different cells affect different areas of the model.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=159)** So if we're looking to understand where our circular reference comes in, we can click and see what does Excel have to say about where these circular references are coming up.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=170)** And it'll tell us all the points where we have those circular reference arising.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=176)** So if we were to try and trace where our circular reference problem comes from, it looks like it comes from the fact that we're using the same information at the bottom of the sheet and then the top of the sheet.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=190)** Let me show you what I mean.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=192)** So when we look at the level of interest paid on debt and we trace precedents and dependents, we see that the level of debt, interest paid on debt falls into our net interest income.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=208)** Net interest income, however, is also used up here in the top of the sheet for interest income.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=216)** In fact, interest income specifically says that it's simply supposed to be the net interest income from the bottom of the sheet.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=227)** But pre-tax income is based on our interest income, which in turn, affects net income, which affects cashflow from operations, which affects free cashflow, et cetera.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=238)** So it creates a large circle overall.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=240)** How do we break that circle?
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=243)** Well, there's no perfect way to do this.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=247)** But one of the best ways is to simply say, let's take the net interest income that the model predicts here at the bottom and let's apply that to the interest income line at the top by copying and pasting it from the bottom and then typing Alt and holding down Alt, type E as in Elephant, S as in Special, V as in Values.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=277)** This lets us paste values in for the interest income rather than using a formula.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=282)** Now, normally, we would never wanna do this.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=285)** Why? Well, because it makes the sheet less dynamic.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=288)** But in this case, we have a circular reference.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=290)** And so in order to avoid problems with the output on the sheet, it's helpful to paste special values in here.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=296)** The key is, if we now make changes to the model, we have to remember to come back and update this particular line.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=304)** Adding a comment box to the sheet can help us do that.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=309)** We can add the comment box by coming over to the Review tab, then clicking New Comment.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=316)** And I'll simply say, Update this line when model changes to avoid circular reference problems.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=330)** All of this has helped to make our model more readable and more effective for use in decision making.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/hiding-tabs-and-making-models-readable?u=76281980&t=337)** Now, we've seen how to hide sheets and rows, which should allow you build more elegant and readable financial models going forward.

#### Stress testing models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Using financial models in business requires being able to show what will happen when unexpected events arise.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=6)** And that's exactly what stress testing is all about.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=9)** Now we have here an LBO model related to the buyout of a firm.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=14)** We've made a variety of different assumptions.
>

>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=25)** That is, if the share price is $140 per share we will pay a 20% premium and offer $168 per share.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=35)** We're gonna use 50% debt and 50% equity.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=38)** We can change these number of course any time we'd like, and a good financial model should update.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=44)** At the end of the day what we really care about is how profitable this particular investment will be.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=50)** We can measure that in a variety of ways.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=53)** Net present value and IROR, or internal rate of return, are two of the most common.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=58)** IROR is arguably more popular within finance, particularly within corporate finance, largely because it provides essentially a rate of return, a percentage interest rate, and we can compare that to other choices or other investments we have.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=76)** So if you notice, depending on the changing assumptions that we make, our IROR will go up or down.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=83)** The more debt we use, the higher our IROR becomes.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=87)** But, let's ask ourselves what's the limit on using debt?
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=92)** Well the answer is we might be concerned about defaults.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=95)** Now, we've assumed that we're going to have economic scenario two play out.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=101)** What is economic scenario two?
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=104)** Well, this relates to a hidden sheet that we have in the model.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=110)** The Acme three statement sheet.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=113)** Scenario two as the Acme sheet shows is our base case scenario.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=119)** The gray sky scenario is scenario one, the blue sky scenario is scenario three.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=125)** We hid the Acme sheet to make it a little easier to read, but as we change these parameters, as we change the economic scenario, our IROR goes up or down.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=138)** If we change to scenario three, the blue sky scenario, our IROR becomes 42%.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=146)** If we change to the gray sky scenario, our IROR says pound number sign.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=154)** What this tell us is that the IROR is negative, we will actually lose money on this particular investment.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=161)** Where does that IROR come from?
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=163)** Well it's from cell H 138 in our sheet.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=168)** So looking down, we see that if we have a gray sky scenario in this particular case, we'll actually lose money on the deal overall.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=179)** Any of the other two scenarios will give us a positive return.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=183)** But, we can also use the financial model for more than just assessing what the return will be on that investment.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=189)** We might be interested in what will happen to our level of cash along the way.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=195)** Are we at risk of defaulting on our debt?
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=198)** So in this particular case we're using 70% debt, 30% equity, and if we scroll down, we'll figure out where the debt comes in.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=210)** So our ending cash balance, row 110 shows us the amount of cash that we have on hand.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=217)** We start with 23 million, 363 thousand in cash at the end of the first year.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=223)** If we change to a gray sky scenario, what happens?
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=229)** So changing economic scenario to number one in cell F6.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=235)** And then if we scroll down again, what we see is the end of the first year, now we have 15 million, 56 thousand in cash.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=245)** We burn through cash from operations, we're losing money on that.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=250)** We're not issuing additional debt, presumably we've already tapped out all the debt we can use, so as a result we end 2019 with only 2.2 million in cash on hand, and in 2020, we will default on our debt.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=264)** We will have negative ending cash, which means we won't be able to make interest payments to the bank as expected.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=272)** This is a good example of how we can use scenario analysis in a financial model to look for problems that are coming down the road.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/stress-testing-models?u=76281980&t=282)** Now you're ready to start stress-testing financial models that you build on your own.
