### 7. 6. Buyout Models

#### Interest rate assumptions in models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=0)** Interest rates are often a critical element in financial models.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=3)** They drive cost of debt which is a major expense that determines business outcomes.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=8)** Figuring out reasonable interest rates to use can be tricky though.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=12)** Now we have this firm that we're considering a buyout for.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=15)** And the firm has 100 million dollars in debt.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=20)** We're assuming that the interest rate will be 8% on that debt.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=25)** And then the annual interest rate change will be 1% per year.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=29)** That is, rates will rise by 1% each year.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=32)** However, If we change these assumptions.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=35)** It's pretty easy to see that the net income shown here will change.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=40)** So, if we instead cut interest rates so they're only rising half a percent per year our net income now goes up to 8.3 million dollars versus the 5.3 it was before.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=54)** Similarly if our interest rate is only 6% or 5%, instead of 8%, net incomes are also higher.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=64)** So, what's a reasonable interest rate to use?
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=67)** Well to answer that question, we'd really like to draw on outside data.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=71)** To do that, FRED can be a great tool.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=75)** So I'm gonna create a new sheet here and we're going to use FRED data based on the FRED Excel add-in.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=82)** So I'll come over to the FRED tab after enabling the add-in as we've seen previously.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=89)** And I'm gonna come down to money, banking and finance.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=93)** And there's a variety of different metrics for interest rates I could use.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=97)** The Fed funds rate, treasury rates at different years, et cetera.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=102)** I'm probably most interested in corporate bond rates.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=106)** Either the rates from S&P for double A or triple B companies that is strong investment grade and strong junk bond companies.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=114)** Or, the Moody's rates for triple A and BAA, again, strong investment grade and strong junk grade bonds.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=123)** I'm gonna go ahead and I'll use the Moody's debt ratings.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=127)** So I click the Moody's debt rating and I'm gonna click get FRED data and what we'll see is that interest rates have varied considerable over time.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=138)** In 1986, interest rates were as high as 11.4%.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=142)** Whereas, as of the end of May 2018, interest rates for junk bonds were more like 4.7%.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=149)** So they varied dramatically over time.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=152)** Already though, we've discovered something important.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=155)** Probably the maximum interest rate that we would end up paying is around 11%.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=162)** The minimum interest rate that we would end up paying is probably around 4 to 5%.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=168)** Something like that.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=170)** The level of interest however makes a significant difference.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=174)** At an 11% interest rate we make just less than a million dollars in net income per year.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=180)** With a 4% interest rate our net income balloons to more than 10 times that.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=186)** So It's important to understand what reasonable levels of interest rates are.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=191)** Now, we might ask ourselves what's the average over time?
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=194)** Well we can compute that fairly easily in Excel using an average if function.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=202)** So I'm gonna use the average if function just to avoid problems with some of the missing values here.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=209)** So I've highlighted my entire row and then clicked shift, control, down in order to get all of the values that I'm interested in.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=219)** So that's my range.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=221)** Now, what's my criteria?
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=223)** Well I'm simply going to say that I'm looking for any interest rates greater than 0%.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=228)** After all, you can't have a negative interest rate and this'll help to throw out all of those NA values.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=234)** Once I do that I can close my parentheses up and I see that over time, the average interest rate for a strong junk bond company has been about 7.43%.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=247)** If I just change this to a number by hitting control one and then make it two decimal places we see that the average interest rate is 7.43%.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=257)** Now, obviously that's come down over time but in our particular case maybe we'd want to use an interest rate like say 6% with a half a percentage point increase if we think that interest rates are going to reverse their long term course.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=274)** Based on that interest rate we could now change our scenarios and see what happens to net income under a variety of circumstances.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=284)** Whatever specific financial model we've used though we now have a reasonable basis for deciding what our interest rates are going to be in the future.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/interest-rate-assumptions-in-models?u=76281980&t=296)** Now that you've seen how to use historical data on interest rates to provide a sanity check on your own assumptions, you're ready to put this into practice with your own financial models.

#### Discount rates in models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Interest rates and other factors play directly in the financial models in many cases, but they can also indirectly influence a value that we call WACC or waited average cost of capitol.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=12)** WACC is essentially the cost that a company pays on average to raise money for any sort of project or investment.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=20)** It's a combination of the cost of equity and the cost of debt.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=25)** One of the key determinants for whether we should proceed with a particular project is whether our IRR in a particular project is greater than the WACC in that project, which is generally represented by the discount rate.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=42)** In our model here, we're using a discount rate of 9%, which is correlated to the WACC for the company in question or the investment in question.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=52)** Now, what impact does WACC have on the valuation of this project?
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=58)** Well we can see very simply, if we change discount rate from say 9% to 10%, that the implied price per share in the future on this investment falls.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=68)** If we take the discount rate up to say 12%, price per share goes from 243 to 214.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=75)** In other words, the cost of obtaining capitol for this particular investment is related to the ultimate return on that investment.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=86)** The higher the cost obtaining capitol, the lower the return on the investment eventually.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=91)** We can create a table that tells us how sensitive our investment is to the relative level of our discount rate or our WACC.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=101)** We've done that here.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=102)** First, we have the terminal growth rate method.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=107)** This method looks at using the terminal growth rate, in this case assumed to be 2%, and the discount rate in order to figure out what the price per share of this investment should be.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=120)** The way to read this table is that with a terminal growth rate of 3% and a discount rate of 9%, our price per share should be $279.69 at the end of the investment period, that is in five years time.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=139)** Using no growth, that is 0% growth and a 6% discount rate, our price per share is $306 and it can get as high as $963 per share if we have a 5% terminal growth rate and a 6% WACC.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=157)** On the other side of the coin, if our discount rate is very high, in this case 12% and we have no growth, price per share could be as low as $203.54.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=169)** We can do a similar analysis based on discount rate and the terminal EBITDA to enterprise value multiple.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=179)** We've assumed that to be seven times, here at the top of the sheet.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=184)** So again, looking at this table, what we observe is that with a seven times EBITDA multiple and a 9% WACC, the EBITDA method says the value per share should be $262.91.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=199)** That ranges from as little as $192.01, with a very high discount rate and a very low multiple, to as much as $391 with a high multiple and a low discount rate.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/discount-rates-in-models?u=76281980&t=217)** Now we've seen how WACC influences a firm's valuation through the discount rate and the decisions that we make based on that particular rate and the outcomes it would produce for our investment.

#### Top-down financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's common to assume that our firm's financial results could best be illustrated with a CAGR, or compounded annual growth rate.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=9)** The reason this is done in industry is not necessarily because it's correct, but because it's easy.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=14)** An alternative approach is to use a top-down model for forecasting future financial results.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=21)** Now, what we see is that this particular model has a 10% CAGR built-in and that CAGR changes based on the scenario in question.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=31)** For negative scenarios, like a recession, we switch to a 15% CAGR and for very rosy scenarios, we switch to a 20% CAGR, or compounded annual growth rate.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=43)** This a rough, blunt tool.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=47)** It's unlikely that we know exactly what our compounded annual growth rate will be and it's unlikely that we'll have exact, the same compounded annual growth rate over time or that that compounded annual growth rate will change by the same amount every year.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=64)** Say, rising or falling 1%.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=66)** Instead, we might wanna use a little bit more sophisticated approach, like a top-down financial forecasting model.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=74)** To do that, I'm gonna scroll down and we'll have to make a few assumptions.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=80)** Now, notice the bottom line that we care about is this revenue growth line, row 42, but in order to get to row 42, we're gonna have to make some other assumptions.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=92)** In particular, we're gonna have to figure out how much we think market sales will grow every year, how much we think our share of the market will grow every year, how much we think that our order value will change each year.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=108)** Those three can then be combined to determine what the top-down growth rate is expected to be.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=116)** For the year 2018, I've started us out by assuming 3% total market share growth, that is the total market, the size of the pie, gross 3%, our particular market share, gross 1%, our piece of the pie increases by 1%, and then the order value, that is how much customers spend with us, grows by 6%.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=141)** Notice, total market sales and market share are both in unit sales.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=147)** Order value is in dollars.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=150)** We don't have to do it that way, but it's helpful to break these things out over time.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=155)** Now, you might be asking, well, where could I get the right kind of data to help me with this type of forecast?
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=160)** One good answer to that is FRED, the Federal Reserve Economic Database.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=165)** We've talked about that previously.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=168)** In any event, let's assume that we have decided, based on our external data use, what the appropriate level of market share growth holding and let's pretend that we're looking at economic growth over time.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=181)** We think that maybe the economy will be fairly strong for the next couple of years, and so we're projecting 3% growth and then 4% growth, followed by 2% growth and then a recession in 2022.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=195)** That gives us negative 3% growth.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=200)** I'm just going to make these all formatted uniformly.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=203)** Now, we wanna ask how much our share of the pie is growing.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=206)** Well, maybe we think that we're gonna do a good job rolling out some new products and so we'll pick up some market share, particularly in 2020, but then thereafter, we'll be flat.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=218)** Because we're not willing to see price, maybe we'll even lose a little bit of market share in 2022.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=226)** Next, we have to ask how much will our order value grow each year.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=230)** Well, as you probably know, inflation over time dictates that the price of all goods should rise.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=237)** Something that cost $100 last year might cost, as an example, $101 or $102 on average this year.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=246)** That should factor into our order value growth.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=249)** Assuming our particular industry is inline with inflation, we probably oughta see order value growth of at least 2% to 3%.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=257)** That is roughly what the inflation rate is growing at, but ideally we'd like to get our customers to spend more over time with us by rolling out more appealing products and ideally by being able to capture a little bit higher price because we offer a premium product.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=272)** I will assume that we can grow our order value by, say, 5%, 6% for the first couple of years, and then, after that, maybe it falls to the rate of inflation.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=284)** Once we've done that, we're prepared to expand the top-down growth rate formula and we see that our top-down growth rate is expected to be about 10.3% for the first few years and then it falls to 4% and finally negative 3%.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=301)** Now, let's see how this impacts profits by using the toggle shown above.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=306)** If we change from using a CAGR growth rate to the top-down growth rate by typing a one, what happens to our net income?
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=316)** Net income falls in this particular case.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=319)** Now, of course, we'd rather a higher net income, but that's not the objective of using top-down growth versus CAGRs.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=327)** Instead, our goal is to get a more accurate picture of what net income will be in the future.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=333)** Top-down growth rates, if we've done it correctly, should be more accurate and should give us a more realistic view on what might happen going forward.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/top-down-financial-models?u=76281980&t=342)** Now, you're ready to apply top-down methods to build more accurate financial models for your own purposes.

#### Bottom-up financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An alternative to top-down models of financial forecasting are bottom-up analysis.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=6)** Now, we've gone through when we've added a toggle because we now have a few different methods for forecasting how revenue growth will change over time.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=14)** So if we type in a 1, that indicates the top-down method, if we type in a 2, that's gonna be for our bottom-up method, and 3 will be for our conventional simple and easy to use CAGR method.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=27)** And as we see, at present, we're using the top-down method.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=31)** If we instead switch to the CAGR method, we see that that flows through and our net income at the end of the period does in fact change.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=39)** Now, we're interested in trying to use a bottom-up method as an alternative.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=45)** So while the top-down method starts with target sales and then filters through market share and then order value, in order to get overall growth rates, the bottom-up analysis starts by looking at what is our firm's traffic.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=60)** And then from there what is the conversion rate based on our traffic, and then how much can we increase prices over time.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=68)** But the calculation will be similar in each case.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=71)** Now, whether we're using a top-down approach or a bottom-up approach, this method is more complex and more difficult than a CAGR because we're gonna have to draw an outside data.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=83)** It's hopefully more accurate because we're not using a simple CAGR method, but it does take more time and effort.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=90)** Again, if you're looking for sources of data, Fred is a great place to start, but there's a variety of options out there.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=98)** So let's pretend that we have looked at some external data and we've made some forecasts about how much we think that customer traffic, or potential customer traffic will grow over time.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=111)** So the number of people coming through our stores or to our website should grow at least in line with the overall population, right?
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=120)** So we'd wanna look at population growth within our particular markets.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=123)** That might sound trivial, but if we're talking about a business space in, say, the Northeast US, where population is roughly stagnant, we might only have, say, 0.1% per year population growth.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=136)** In contrast, in some fast growing areas, like, say the Southeast, population rises by one or even 1 1/2% per year.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=145)** So prospect traffic should grow by at least to that amount.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=149)** Now in addition, hopefully, we're going to be able to increase marketing over time and that'll increase the level of growth that we have in terms of people coming through the door, right?
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=159)** So perhaps, we're gonna increase our marketing over time, and combining that with population growth, and GDP growth overall, maybe we think that prospect traffic will slowly ramp up.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=172)** So it's gonna start at 2% and then we forecast that it'll rise to 3%, 4%, 5%, before it falls back in the case of a recession, and customer traffic will now be down 8%.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=189)** Again, we're forecasting a recession five years out.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=193)** With our basic CAGR, we don't have the power to do this directly.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=198)** Now, on top of that, we've gotta ask about our conversion growth.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=203)** Well, our conversion rate just says, out of every hundred people that come through the door to our store, what percentage of people buy?
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=211)** That might stay constant over time or it might increase.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=214)** The conversion is going to depend heavily on the kind of industry we're in.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=218)** If we're offering a better product though, presumably, we ought to be able to convert people, maybe a little bit better over time.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=225)** So we'll assume, maybe, 1% conversion growth the first couple of years, followed by a 1/2 a percent, and then, no conversion rate growth.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=236)** And we're gonna lose 1% when the recession hits.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=239)** Similarly, we wanna put in place price growth, and maybe that starts with only being the level of inflation.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=247)** And then from there, we're able to get a little bit of pricing power and so we ramp up our price and then we hold it roughly constant in line with inflation after that.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=258)** So our bottom-up growth rate then varies anywhere from -7.1% in our recession situation, to +9.7%, we have a strong economy, and we're putting a lot more money into marketing.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=273)** How does this impact our net income?
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=275)** Well, let's take a look.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=277)** If we switch our forecast method, from the top-down method to a bottom-up method, we see that net income falls.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=287)** Again, the goal here is not to create scenarios or forecast methods to artificially boost net income, it is to hopefully create a more accurate picture of what net income will ultimately be in the future based on our projections.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/bottom-up-financial-models?u=76281980&t=306)** Now, you're ready to apply bottoms-up methods to building out more accurate financial forecast for yourself.

#### IRR decisions in financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Making decisions in business is the ultimate goal of a financial model.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=4)** There's no point in a model if it doesn't lead to better decision making by the firm, right?
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=10)** To make those decisions, business professionals often turn to IRR's or Internal Rates of Return, which are derived from financial models and the projections they produce.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=22)** Now, we're at the bottom of the sheet, where we ultimately have the level of cash that is going to be paid out to investors.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=29)** And as row 136 shows us, is that, initially, in 2017, we invest 86,254,000 dollars in this particular firm, in buying out for LBO.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=44)** Every year after that, we begin to pay ourselves small dividends that ramp up over time, so in 2018, we pay ourselves a 300,000 dollar dividend, 2019, it's a 400,000, and then a 500,000, and finally, a 600,000 dollar dividend.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=59)** In 2022, after we've improved the firm, we're ready to turn around and sell the company.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=66)** Now, we project, based on all the financial modeling that we have done above, that we will be able to sell the firm for 214.1819 million dollars, so maybe 214 and change, right?
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=81)** Now, we need to compute an IRR for that.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=84)** Our financial model can do that quite easily.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=87)** As we see in row 138, to compute our IRR, we just take the level of cash flows throughout this period, and put them into the IRR formula, and this spits out a 20.3 percent IRR.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=103)** There are a number of factors that are gonna influence this IRR, but of course, the biggest ones are: what are the levels of cash flows over time, how much do we pay up front, and then how much do we receive over time?
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=118)** In order to evaluate what our IRR will be, under a variety of circumstances, we can produce some sensitivity analysis charts.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=127)** So this sensitivity analysis chart up top shows us, based on a particular price per share that we buy the stock at, and the exit multiple, that is how much we can sell the company for in terms of a multiple on its EBITDA, at the end of that period, what our IRR's are expected to be.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=148)** So at a 10 times multiple, for example, if we pay a 25 percent premium for the stock, or 175 dollars per share, that produces an IRR of 18.8 percent.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=162)** On the low end, if we can only get a six times multiple, and we have to pay a 45 percent premium on the stock, for 203 dollars per share, then we'll only produce an IRR of .4 percent.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=176)** On the high end, if we can get a very high multiple, 14 times, and we don't have to pay any premium on the stock, so we can buy it for 140 dollars per share, this results in an IRR of 35.4 percent.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=189)** We can do a similar computation based on another factor that influences our IRR, the level of debt that we have.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=198)** The more debt we use, the higher the IRR will be on our equity investment, but of course, the greater the risk of something going wrong along the way, and the firm going bankrupt.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=213)** Hopefully, at this point now, you're ready to begin making modeling decisions built around IRR's for your own firm.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/irr-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=220)** Producing these kinds of sensitivity charts is very useful for corporate executives because it shows them what the range of possible outcomes are, given important assumptions, like the exit multiple and the level of debt that'll be used in the buy-out.

#### NPV decisions in financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An alternative decision making criteria in financial modeling is NPV, or net present value.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=6)** It's a bit less intuitive, but it has some critical advantages over IRR when making a major decision.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=13)** Now, we've set up two decision criteria, IRR, internal rate of return, and NPV or net present value, and we pull each of these different metrics from different parts of our overall advanced valuation model.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=29)** In particular net present value comes out of the DCF model combined with some data from the LBL portion of the model and IRR comes from the LBL portion of the model.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=40)** Now, we can change the scenario that we're using.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=44)** So if we use, as an example, scenario three for our growth, which is the Blue Sky case, we have a very high IRR, 36 percent, and our NPV is a positive 236.6 million dollars.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=60)** The criteria for net present value, is shown here.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=64)** If our net present value is bigger than zero, we should go ahead with the project.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=69)** Net present value tells us the profit that we will generate in present value dollars from doing a particular investment.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=80)** If this number is positive, we wanna go forward with the investment.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=85)** We compare IRR to our discount rate.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=88)** If our IRR is higher than the discount rate then we go forward with the project.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=93)** So IRR requires a little bit more manipulation.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=97)** Simply having a positive IRR is not sufficient to tell us whether or not we should go forward.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=103)** In this case our IRR is compared against the discount rate In this case our IRR is compared against the discount rate shown in our DCF model of nine percent.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=115)** Now, what happens if we have an alternative, less positive scenario?
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=120)** Well, the top scenario, the Blue Sky scenario was scenario three as we see here.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=126)** Our base case is scenario two.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=129)** Now our IRR falls to just under 24 percent.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=133)** But we should still go ahead with the investment.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=135)** And our net present value is 134 million.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=138)** Again, we should still go ahead with the investment.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=141)** Ideally, IRR and NPV should both tell us the same thing, go forward with this investment.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=148)** Finally, in our Gray Sky scenario, our pessimistic scenario, what happens?
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=155)** Well now our IRR is -21.63 percent, Well now our IRR is -21.63 percent, and the net present value is negative 13 million.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=165)** So both of these decision criteria are telling us not to go forward with this particular investment.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=171)** If we built a robust financial model that pulls all of the data together in one place then determining what decision we should make on this investment is a straightforward and simple task.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/npv-decisions-in-financial-models?u=76281980&t=184)** Now you're ready to make modeling decisions built around NPV.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** irr (14), npv (5), dcf (2), lbl (2)
> **Code Keywords:** return, (1), case, (1), finally, (1)
> **Versions:** 21.63 (2), 236.6 (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Prerequisites:** set up (1)

#### Limits of financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] It's important to understand the limitations on financial models, and when you may run into problems trying to use them.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=7)** The reality is that financial models are a tool.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=10)** They're only as good as the user and the developer of that tool.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=15)** If we make an error in developing the model, or an error in interpreting the output from that model, well we're going to run into problems.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=24)** So what kind of errors may come up?
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=26)** Well, there are four particular areas you wanna pay attention to when it comes to financial models.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=32)** First, is the risk of technical errors, errors related to the development of the model.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=39)** These kinds of errors are technical in nature, and often result from the financial analyst going a little bit too quickly in trying to build out that model.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=47)** The second type of issue you wanna pay attention to are inaccurate assumptions.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=52)** Even if we have a 100% technically perfect model, inaccurate assumptions, can still lead to bad output, and thus bad decision making around that model.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=63)** The third issue relates to unforeseen circumstances.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=66)** Even if our assumptions are reasonable, and our model is technically perfect, we may still end up with events that we hadn't seen coming, that cause the model to be wrong.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=77)** Now of course there will always be some sort of unforeseen circumstances, right?
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=83)** The financial model is a simplification of reality.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=86)** So it's never going to capture everything that will actually happen.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=90)** The key is to make sure that we capture all of the circumstances which are relevant to the business at hand, or at least crucial issues that are relevant to the business at hand.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=101)** And then finally, we wanna pay attention to issues that the modeler might have missed, problems that may crop up.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=108)** In the area of technical errors, there are three common problems that may arise.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=112)** The first are circular references.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=115)** When you have a circular reference, Excel will generally warn you as soon as you open the model.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=120)** Then you wanna go and try and track down that circular reference, and figure out how to resolve it.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=126)** You can typically do this directly through Excel.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=130)** Don't let a circular reference go untouched though.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=133)** If you do, Excel will often have problems producing output, and the output that you see, may actually be wrong.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=142)** Excel's math is often wrong when technical errors are involved, and it'll be obviously wrong, if you start paying attention to the details.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=152)** The second type of technical error you might run into, are errors stemming from overly complicated formulas.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=158)** It's tempting to have nested IF statements, and a variety of other long formulas built into your financial model.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=167)** They make things seem cleaner up front.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=169)** But if you're trying to go through and disect the model, and figure out what's going on, those kinds of long, overly complicated formulas, will in reality end up being the bane of your existence.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=181)** They will make it much more difficult to track down any problems that come up, or to make changes to the model in the future.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=187)** Third are errors based on inaccurate entry of historical data.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=192)** Almost all financial models start with some kind of historical data, either related to a particular company, or related to the macro environment.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=200)** In order to avoid problems with our model, we need to make sure that historical data is accurate, and if we've simply typed something in wrong, we'll have problems.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=210)** The second type of issue that comes up in financial modeling are inaccurate assumptions.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=215)** This is perhaps the easiest problem to have with a model.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=219)** Your assumptions will be wrong, there's no doubt about that.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=223)** If you assume that the interest rate will be 5%, it will not be 5%.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=228)** But the question is, how wrong are you going to be?
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=232)** An assumption of 5% interest rates is probably reasonable.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=235)** It'll be wrong, but not that wrong.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=238)** An assumption of 25% interest rates, at least in the United States, is going to be very, very wrong, and will create undo problems with your model.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=249)** The key here is to stress test your models.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=252)** Try a range of possible assumptions, a range of possible inputs, and see if that alters the decisions that are made based on the model.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=261)** The third type of problem that comes up are unforeseen circumstances.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=266)** So for example, your model could lead to over-optimism about circumstances.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=272)** Is there a competitor that might come into the space that you hadn't counted on?
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=275)** A recession emerging that you hadn't thought about?
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=278)** Or some sort of a force majeure, insured event type situation, like a factory fire, that you haven't accounted for.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=285)** You wanna try and take these possibilities into account when you're doing stress testing on your model.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=291)** Fourth and finally, there are issues the modeler may have missed.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=294)** So what's the minimum amount of cash needed in the model?
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=298)** For example, can you really go all the way to zero cash?
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=301)** Or do you need to have some level of cash on the balance sheet?
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=304)** Just to deal with trade credit problems and things like that.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=308)** If you assume your cash can go to zero before you default, you may end up missing a crucial event that'll occur for the company.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=317)** Similarly, you need to think about how you're gonna deal with tax treatments or changes.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=322)** US tax code changes all the time.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=324)** How're you gonna deal with issues like net operating losses, NOLs, and other kinds of tax complexities?
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=331)** How are you gonna update your model if the business structure changes?
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=335)** If a particular division or business segment is sold, or a new product is introduced?
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=340)** What impact will that have on your model?
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=343)** Think carefully about these types of issues, and take them into account, because they will have a critical impact on how effective and robust your model is.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=353)** Hopefully now, you understand the limitations on financial models.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/limits-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=357)** While powerful, the financial model is no substitute for clear, big-picture business thinking.

### 8. 7. Financial Modeling across Industries

#### Characteristics of financial models
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are three major types of models you may encounter on a regular basis.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=4)** It's good idea to understand the differences between these models so you can figure out which one is right for your circumstances and the questions you're trying to answer.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=12)** The three major models that you probably care most about are the Three Statement Model, the DCF Model, and the Buyout Model.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=21)** Each of them has different characteristics and helps us to evaluate different questions.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=26)** Now, there's a variety of different types of questions that we might ask around models.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=32)** Could be, we're interested in valuation, could be, we're interested in a measurement of risk based on the model, or could be that we're interested in the range of possible outcomes in the future.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=44)** Each of the models approaches these outcomes in a different way.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=49)** From a valuation perspective, the three statement model allows us to use a present value of our free cashflow or multiples based on the projections of cashflows for the future.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=62)** The DCF model allows us to go through and compute an IRR on a particular asset purchase and then compare that versus a market hurdle rate or discount rate, and the buyout model allows us to look at what our entry multiple and acquisition premium are and compare those based on the IRR and the hurdle rate in question.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=85)** Similarly, we can examine the risks, either from the prospective of an equity investor, a dead investor, or a base case perspective.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=96)** We can do this in a variety of ways.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=98)** For example, we might look at what our cost of capital is, we might look at our sensitivity to particular cashflows based on the assumptions that we might make, or we might look at evaluating what level of assumptions we have to make in order to break even in a particular setting.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=118)** The types of risk analysis that we do are gonna be similar between the three different models but the specifics will differ.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=126)** We'll look at which characteristics of the model allow us to analyze risk and then evaluate that risk based on our perspective, are we an equity investor, a dead investor, or company management.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=140)** An alternative to this, of course, is Monte Carlo analysis.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=143)** Monte Carlo analysis, effectively an experiment in finance, lets us create a hypothetical set of outcomes based on whatever we think the possible range of outcomes are.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=157)** So if we say the maximum possible cashflow from this investment is a million dollars and the minimum is a loss of a million dollars, well Monte Carlo lets us go through an experiment to see what's the most likely outcome.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=170)** And we can do that under any of the three models.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=173)** All three models have different characteristics.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=176)** In the three statement model, we're gonna start by examining what kinds of historical information we have and what are the drivers of our cash outcomes over time.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=188)** What are the drivers of earnings, of sales, of cost, et cetera?
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=193)** In a DCF model, we might also use our historical financial statements but we're gonna rely more heavily on an analysis of what the future may hold so we're more likely to use something like a simple CAGR to estimate what our sales are likely to be in the future.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=210)** The buyout model does something similar.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=213)** Again, we'll use historical financial statements but we're gonna drill down based on valuation changes that we might make if we're investing in the business.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=223)** Is there an opportunity to create value, something that present management has overlooked.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=227)** Each of these models is gonna really on different pieces of information.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=231)** Whether it's the sources and uses cashflow statements, whether it's historical balance sheets, or whether it's going to be a combination of different cashflow statements like the balance sheet, the cashflow statement, and the income statement.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=246)** At the end of the day, the goal in all three models, is to go thru an estimate, what's gonna happen to that firm in the future, so that we could then evaluate whether our debt is sustainable, what the levels of cashflow might be, and what the value will be on this particular asset, or investment at the end of some period of time.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=270)** The outputs vary, just like the models do.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=273)** So we may use IRRs, we may use NPVs, we can use a combination of those metrics.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=281)** The type of model that you're using doesn't matter as much as the type of questions you wanna ask around it.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=288)** In theory, any of these three models can be used in a variety of circumstances.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/financial-modeling-foundations-2018/characteristics-of-financial-models?u=76281980&t=293)** The key is, to make sure you put together a model that you understand how to do, and that will be simple and readable for other users.
